      <p><b>Belarus</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[a]</a></sup> officially the <b>Republic of Belarus</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[b]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to the east and northeast, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> to the west, and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> to the northwest. Belarus covers an area of 207,600 square kilometres (80,200&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), with a population of 9.4 million, and is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_European_countries_by_area" title="List of European countries by area">thirteenth-largest</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_European_countries_by_population" title="List of European countries by population">twentieth-most populous country</a> in Europe. The country is administratively divided into <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Belarus" title="Regions of Belarus">seven regions</a>, and is one of the world's most <a href="/wiki/Urbanization_by_country" title="Urbanization by country">urbanized</a>, with over 40% of its total land area <a href="/wiki/Forested" class="mw-redirect" title="Forested">forested</a>. <a href="/wiki/Minsk" title="Minsk">Minsk</a> is the country's capital and largest city. </p>
      <p>Until the 20th century, different states at various times controlled the lands of modern-day Belarus, including <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Polotsk" title="Principality of Polotsk">Principality of Polotsk</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>. In the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a> in 1917, different states arose competing for legitimacy amidst the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Civil War</a>, ultimately ending in the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">Byelorussian SSR</a>, which became a founding <a href="/wiki/Republics_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Republics of the Soviet Union">constituent republic</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in 1922. After the <a href="/wiki/Polish-Soviet_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish-Soviet War">Polish-Soviet War</a>, Belarus lost almost half of its territory to Poland. Much of the borders of Belarus took their modern shape in 1939, when some lands of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a> were reintegrated into it after the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Poland" title="Soviet invasion of Poland">Soviet invasion of Poland</a>, and were finalized after World War&nbsp;II.<sup id="cite_ref-uni1_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni1-14">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uni2_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni2-15">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uni3_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni3-16">[14]</a></sup> During WWII, military operations devastated Belarus, which lost about a quarter of its population and half of its economic resources.<sup id="cite_ref-axell_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-axell-17">[15]</a></sup> The republic was redeveloped in the post-war years. In 1945, the Byelorussian SSR became a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, along with the Soviet Union. </p>
      <p>The parliament of the republic proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> of Belarus on <span class="nowrap">27 July</span> 1990, and during the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a>, Belarus declared independence on <span class="nowrap">25 August</span> 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> Following the adoption of a new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Belarus" title="Constitution of Belarus">constitution</a> in 1994, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko" title="Alexander Lukashenko">Alexander Lukashenko</a> was elected Belarus's first president in the country's first and only free <a href="/wiki/1994_Belarusian_presidential_election" title="1994 Belarusian presidential election">election</a> post-independence, serving as president ever since.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> Lukashenko openly acknowledges his style of governance as <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian</a> and human rights groups consider <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Belarus" title="Human rights in Belarus">human rights</a> in the country to be poor.<sup id="cite_ref-amnesty_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty-20">[18]</a></sup> Belarus is the only country in Europe officially using the <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_Belarus" title="Capital punishment in Belarus">death penalty</a>. Lukashenko continued a number of Soviet-era policies, such as <a href="/wiki/State_ownership" title="State ownership">state ownership</a> of large sections of the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Belarus" title="Economy of Belarus">economy</a>. In 2000, Belarus and Russia signed a treaty for greater cooperation, forming the <a href="/wiki/Union_State" title="Union State">Union State</a>. </p>
      <p>Belarus is a <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing country</a> ranking very high in the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>. It has been a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> since its founding; and a member of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">CIS</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Collective_Security_Treaty_Organization" title="Collective Security Treaty Organization">CSTO</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Economic_Union" title="Eurasian Economic Union">EAEU</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, it has shown no aspirations for joining the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> but nevertheless maintains a <a href="/wiki/Belarus%E2%80%93European_Union_relations" title="Belarus–European Union relations">bilateral relationship with the Union</a>, and likewise participates in two EU projects: the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Partnership" title="Eastern Partnership">Eastern Partnership</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baku_Initiative" title="Baku Initiative">Baku Initiative</a>. </p> 
      <div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading">

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Etymology_of_Belarus" title="Etymology of Belarus">Etymology of Belarus</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The name <i>Belarus</i> is closely related with the term <i>Belaya Rus<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span></i>, i.e., <i><a href="/wiki/White_Rus%27" class="mw-redirect" title="White Rus'">White Rus'</a></i>. There are several claims to the origin of the name <i>White Rus'</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zaprudnik_1993_2_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zaprudnik_1993_2-21">[19]</a></sup> An ethno-religious theory suggests that the name used to describe the part of old <a href="/wiki/Ruthenia" title="Ruthenia">Ruthenian</a> lands within the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a> that had been populated mostly by <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a> who had been Christianized early, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Black_Ruthenia" title="Black Ruthenia">Black Ruthenia</a>, which was predominantly inhabited by pagan <a href="/wiki/Balts" title="Balts">Balts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> An alternative explanation for the name comments on the white clothing worn by the local Slavic population.<sup id="cite_ref-Zaprudnik_1993_2_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zaprudnik_1993_2-21">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> A third theory suggests that the old Rus' lands that were not conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatars</a> (i.e., Polotsk, Vitebsk and Mogilev) had been referred to as <i>White Rus'</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zaprudnik_1993_2_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zaprudnik_1993_2-21">[19]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Stemp_Efrasinia_Polackaja.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Stemp_Efrasinia_Polackaja.jpg/180px-Stemp_Efrasinia_Polackaja.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Stemp_Efrasinia_Polackaja.jpg/270px-Stemp_Efrasinia_Polackaja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Stemp_Efrasinia_Polackaja.jpg/360px-Stemp_Efrasinia_Polackaja.jpg 2x" data-file-width="456" data-file-height="629"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Stemp_Efrasinia_Polackaja.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Stamp with the <a href="/wiki/Cross_of_Saint_Euphrosyne" title="Cross of Saint Euphrosyne">Cross of St. Euphrosyne</a> by <a href="/wiki/Lazar_Bohsha" title="Lazar Bohsha">Lazar Bohsha</a> from 1992
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The name <i>Rus</i> is often conflated with its Latin forms <i>Russia</i> and <i>Ruthenia</i>, thus Belarus is often referred to as <i>White Russia</i> or <i>White Ruthenia</i>. The name first appeared in <a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German_literature" title="Middle High German literature">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin_literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Latin literature">Latin</a> <a href="/wiki/Medieval_literature" title="Medieval literature">medieval literature</a>; the chronicles of <a href="/wiki/Jan_of_Czarnk%C3%B3w" title="Jan of Czarnków">Jan of Czarnków</a> mention the imprisonment of Lithuanian grand duke <a href="/wiki/Jogaila" class="mw-redirect" title="Jogaila">Jogaila</a> and his mother at "<i lang="la" title="Latin-language text">Albae Russiae, Poloczk dicto</i>" in 1381.<sup id="cite_ref-vauchez_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vauchez-24">[22]</a></sup> The first known use of <i>White Russia</i> to refer to Belarus was in the late-16th century by Englishman Sir <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Horsey" title="Jerome Horsey">Jerome Horsey</a>, who was known for his close contacts with the Russian Royal Court.<sup id="cite_ref-alies_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alies-25">[23]</a></sup> During the 17th century, the Russian <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">tsars</a> used <i>White Rus</i> to describe the lands added from the Grand Duchy of Lithuania.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The term <i>Belorussia</i> (Russian: <span lang="ru">Белору́ссия</span>, the latter part similar but spelled and stressed differently from Росси́я, <i>Russia</i>) first rose in the days of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>, and the Russian Tsar was usually styled "the Tsar of All the Russias", as <i>Russia</i> or the <i>Russian Empire</i> was formed by three parts of Russia—the <a href="/wiki/Great_Russia" title="Great Russia">Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_Russia" title="Little Russia">Little</a>, and <a href="/wiki/White_Ruthenia" title="White Ruthenia">White</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> This asserted that the territories are all Russian and all the peoples are also Russian; in the case of the Belarusians, they were variants of the Russian people.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik Revolution">Bolshevik Revolution</a> in 1917, the term <i>White Russia</i> caused some confusion, as it was also the name of the military force that opposed the red Bolsheviks.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> During the period of the Byelorussian SSR, the term <i>Byelorussia</i> was embraced as part of a national consciousness. In western Belarus under Polish control, <i>Byelorussia</i> became commonly used in the regions of <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82ystok" title="Białystok">Białystok</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grodno" title="Grodno">Grodno</a> during the interwar period.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The term <i>Byelorussia</i> (its names in other languages such as English being based on the Russian form) was only used officially until 1991. Officially, the full name of the country is <i>Republic of Belarus</i> (<span lang="be" title="Belarusian-language text">Рэспубліка Беларусь</span>, <span lang="ru" title="Russian-language text">Республика Беларусь</span>, <i lang="be-Latn" title="Belarusian-language text">Respublika Belarus</i> <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Be-Republic_of_Belarus.oga" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/Be-Republic_of_Belarus.oga" class="internal" title="Be-Republic of Belarus.oga">listen</a></span>&nbsp;<small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Be-Republic_of_Belarus.oga" title="File:Be-Republic of Belarus.oga"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-bynamelaw_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bynamelaw-31">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> In Russia, the usage of <i>Belorussia</i> still is very common.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> In Lithuanian, besides <i>Baltarusija</i> (White Russia), Belarus is also called <i>Gudija</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Belarus" title="History of Belarus">History of Belarus</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Early Slavs</a>
      </div> 
      <p>From 5000 to 2000 BC, <a href="/wiki/Bandkeramik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bandkeramik">Bandkeramik</a> cultures predominated. In addition, remains from the <a href="/wiki/Dnieper-Donets_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Dnieper-Donets culture">Dnieper-Donets culture</a> were found in Belarus and parts of <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a> and other pastoralists roamed through the area by 1,000 BC, and by 500 AD, Slavs had taken up residence, which was circumscribed by the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> who roamed its outskirts. Invaders from Asia, among whom were the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avars_(Carpathians)" class="mw-redirect" title="Avars (Carpathians)">Avars</a>, swept through c. 400–600 AD, but were unable to dislodge the Slavic presence.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The region that is now Belarus was first settled by <a href="/wiki/Baltic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic peoples">Baltic</a> tribes in the 3rd century. Around the 5th century, the area was taken over by Slavic tribes. The takeover was partially due to the lack of military coordination of the Balts but the gradual assimilation of the Balts into Slavic culture was peaceful in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Kievan_Rus.27"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kievan_Rus'">Kievan Rus'</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:001_Kievan_Rus%27_Kyivan_Rus%27_Ukraine_map_1220_1240.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/001_Kievan_Rus%27_Kyivan_Rus%27_Ukraine_map_1220_1240.jpg/170px-001_Kievan_Rus%27_Kyivan_Rus%27_Ukraine_map_1220_1240.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/001_Kievan_Rus%27_Kyivan_Rus%27_Ukraine_map_1220_1240.jpg/255px-001_Kievan_Rus%27_Kyivan_Rus%27_Ukraine_map_1220_1240.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/001_Kievan_Rus%27_Kyivan_Rus%27_Ukraine_map_1220_1240.jpg/340px-001_Kievan_Rus%27_Kyivan_Rus%27_Ukraine_map_1220_1240.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2301" data-file-height="2856"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:001_Kievan_Rus%27_Kyivan_Rus%27_Ukraine_map_1220_1240.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Rus'</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_early_East_Slavic_states" class="mw-redirect" title="List of early East Slavic states">principalities</a> before the Mongol and Lithuanian invasions
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the 9th century the territory of modern Belarus became part of <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, a vast East Slavic state ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Rurikid" class="mw-redirect" title="Rurikid">Rurikid</a> dynasty. Upon the death of Kievan Rus' ruler <a href="/wiki/Yaroslav_I_the_Wise" class="mw-redirect" title="Yaroslav I the Wise">Yaroslav I the Wise</a>, the state split into independent principalities.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> Many early Rus' principalities were virtually razed or severely affected by a major <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Rus%27" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongol invasion of Rus'">Mongol invasion</a> in the 13th century, but the lands of modern Belarus avoided the brunt of the invasion and eventually joined the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> There are no sources of military seizure, but the annals affirm the alliance and united foreign policy of <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Polotsk" title="Principality of Polotsk">Polotsk</a> and Lithuania for decades.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> Trying to avoid the <a href="/wiki/Tatar_Yoke" class="mw-redirect" title="Tatar Yoke">Tatar Yoke</a>, the Principality of Minsk sought protection from Lithuanian princes further north and in 1242, <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Minsk" title="Principality of Minsk">Minsk</a> became a part of the expanding Grand Duchy of Lithuania. </p>
      <p>Incorporation into the Grand Duchy of Lithuania resulted in an economic, political and ethno-cultural unification of Belarusian lands.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> Of the principalities held by the Duchy, nine of them were settled by a population that would eventually become Belarusian people.<sup id="cite_ref-zaprudnik_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zaprudnik-43">[41]</a></sup> During this time, the Duchy was involved in several military campaigns, including fighting on the side of Poland against the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Grunwald" title="Battle of Grunwald">Battle of Grunwald</a> in 1410; the joint victory allowed the Duchy to control the northwestern borderlands of Eastern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Muscovites</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III of Moscow</a>, began military campaigns in 1486 in an attempt to incorporate the former lands of Kievan Rus', specifically the territories of modern Belarus, Russia and Ukraine.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Polish.E2.80.93Lithuanian_Commonwealth"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Polish–Lithuanian_Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On 2 February 1386, the Grand Duchy of Lithuania and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland_(1025%E2%80%931385)" title="Kingdom of Poland (1025–1385)">Kingdom of Poland</a> were joined in a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> through a <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Krewo" title="Union of Krewo">marriage of their rulers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> This union set in motion the developments that eventually resulted in the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, created in 1569 by the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Lublin" title="Union of Lublin">Union of Lublin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lukowski_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lukowski-47">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Riasanovsky_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riasanovsky-48">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Lithuanian nobles were forced to go for rapprochement because of the threat coming from <a href="/wiki/Muscovy" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscovy">Muscovy</a>. To strengthen the independence in the format of the union, three editions of the <a href="/wiki/Statutes_of_Lithuania" title="Statutes of Lithuania">Statutes of Lithuania</a> were issued in the 16th century. The third Article of the Statute establishes that all lands of the Grand Duchy of Lithuania will be eternally in the Grand Duchy of Lithuania and never enter as a part of other states. It allowed the right to own land within the Grand Duchy of Lithuania to only its own families. Anyone from outside the Duchy gaining rights to a property would actually own it only after swearing allegiance to the Grand Duke of Lithuania. These articles were aimed to defend the rights of the nobility of the Grand Duchy of Lithuania nobility against Polish, Prussian and other aristocracy of <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In the years following the union, the process of gradual <a href="/wiki/Polonization" title="Polonization">Polonization</a> of both Lithuanians and Ruthenians gained steady momentum. In culture and social life, both the <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish language</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> became dominant, and in 1696, Polish replaced Ruthenian as the official language—with the Ruthenian language being banned from administrative use.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> However, the Ruthenian peasants continued to speak their own language and remained faithful to the <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_Greek_Catholic_Church" title="Belarusian Greek Catholic Church">Belarusian Greek Catholic Church</a>. Statutes were initially issued in the <a href="/wiki/Ruthenian_language" title="Ruthenian language">Ruthenian language</a> alone and later also in Polish. Around 1840 the Statutes were banned by the Russian tsar following the <a href="/wiki/November_Uprising" title="November Uprising">November Uprising</a>. Modern Ukrainian lands used it until 1860s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_Empire">Russian Empire</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ko%C5%9Bciuszko_Uprising" title="Kościuszko Uprising">Kościuszko Uprising</a>, <a href="/wiki/November_Uprising" title="November Uprising">November Uprising</a>, <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">January Uprising</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_history_in_the_Russian_Empire" title="Belarusian history in the Russian Empire">Belarusian history in the Russian Empire</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Berezyna.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Berezyna.jpg/220px-Berezyna.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Berezyna.jpg/330px-Berezyna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Berezyna.jpg/440px-Berezyna.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="702"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Berezyna.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Napoleon's <i>Grande Armée</i> retreating after <a href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_invasion_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon's invasion of Russia">his invasion of Russia</a> and crossing the <a href="/wiki/Berezina_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Berezina river">Berezina river</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Barysaw" title="Barysaw">Barysaw</a>, Belarus)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The union between Poland and Lithuania ended in 1795 with the <a href="/wiki/Third_Partition_of_Poland" title="Third Partition of Poland">Third Partition of Poland</a> by Imperial Russia, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Austria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> The Belarusian territories acquired by the Russian Empire under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Catherine_II_of_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Catherine II of Russia">Catherine&nbsp;II</a><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> were included into the <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_Governorate" title="Belarusian Governorate">Belarusian Governorate</a> (Russian: <span lang="ru" title="Russian-language text">Белорусское генерал-губернаторство</span>) in 1796 and held until their occupation by the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-olson95_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olson95-52">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Under <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Nicholas I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Russia" title="Alexander III of Russia">Alexander III</a> the national cultures were repressed. Policies of <a href="/wiki/Polonization" title="Polonization">Polonization</a><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> changed by <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-zytko-1_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zytko-1-54">[52]</a></sup> which included the return to <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christianity</a> of Belorusian <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Brest" title="Union of Brest">Uniates</a>. Belarusian language was banned in schools while in neighboring <a href="/wiki/Samogitia" title="Samogitia">Samogitia</a> primary school education with <a href="/wiki/Samogitian_dialect" title="Samogitian dialect">Samogitian</a> literacy was allowed.<sup id="cite_ref-Корнилов1908_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Корнилов1908-55">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In a <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a> drive in the 1840s, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Nicholas I</a> prohibited use of the Belarusian language in public schools, campaigned against Belarusian publications and tried to pressure those who had converted to Catholicism under the Poles to reconvert to the Orthodox faith. In 1863, economic and cultural pressure exploded in a <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">revolt</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Konstanty_Kalinowski" title="Konstanty Kalinowski">Konstanty Kalinowski</a>. After the failed revolt, the Russian government reintroduced the use of <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic</a> to Belarusian in 1864 and no documents in Belarusian were permitted by the Russian government until 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the negotiations of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brest-Litovsk_(Russia%E2%80%93Central_Powers)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Brest-Litovsk (Russia–Central Powers)">Treaty of Brest-Litovsk</a>, Belarus first declared independence under German occupation on <span class="nowrap">25 March</span> 1918, forming the <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_People%27s_Republic" title="Belarusian People's Republic">Belarusian People's Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-birgerson_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birgerson-57">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> Immediately afterwards, the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Soviet_War" title="Polish–Soviet War">Polish–Soviet War</a> ignited, and the territory of Belarus was divided between Poland and Soviet Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> The Rada of the Belarusian Democratic Republic exists as a <a href="/wiki/Government_in_exile" title="Government in exile">government in exile</a> ever since then; in fact, it is currently the world's longest serving government in exile.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_states_and_interwar_period">Early states and interwar period</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Caricature_for_Riga_Peace_1921.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Caricature_for_Riga_Peace_1921.png/220px-Caricature_for_Riga_Peace_1921.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Caricature_for_Riga_Peace_1921.png/330px-Caricature_for_Riga_Peace_1921.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Caricature_for_Riga_Peace_1921.png/440px-Caricature_for_Riga_Peace_1921.png 2x" data-file-width="1903" data-file-height="1311"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Caricature_for_Riga_Peace_1921.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Caricature illustrating the partition of Belarus between <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolsheviks</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Riga" title="Peace of Riga">Peace of Riga</a>, 1921
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_People%27s_Republic" title="Belarusian People's Republic">Belarusian People's Republic</a> was the first attempt to create an independent Belarusian state under name "Belarus". Despite significant efforts the state ceased to exist, primarily because the territory was continually dominated by the <a href="/wiki/German_Imperial_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="German Imperial Army">German Imperial Army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Army" title="Imperial Russian Army">Imperial Russian Army</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, and then the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a>. It existed from only 1918 to 1919 but created prerequisites for the formation of the state idea around the name "Belarus". The choice of the name was probably based on the fact that the educated core of the newly formed government was educated in the tsardom universities, with a corresponding education around the ideology of West-Russianism.<sup id="cite_ref-Jr.Zaprudnik2010_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jr.Zaprudnik2010-61">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Central_Lithuania" title="Republic of Central Lithuania">Republic of Central Lithuania</a> was a short-lived political entity, which was the last attempt to restore Lithuania in the historical confederacy state (it was also supposed to create Lithuania Upper and Lithuania Lower). The republic was created in 1920 following <a href="/wiki/%C5%BBeligowski%27s_Mutiny" title="Żeligowski's Mutiny">the staged rebellion</a> of soldiers of the <a href="/wiki/1st_Lithuanian%E2%80%93Belarusian_Division" title="1st Lithuanian–Belarusian Division">1st Lithuanian–Belarusian Division</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish Army">Polish Army</a> under <a href="/wiki/Lucjan_%C5%BBeligowski" title="Lucjan Żeligowski">Lucjan Żeligowski</a>. Centered on the historical capital of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vilna" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilna">Vilna</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>: <i lang="lt">Vilnius</i>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <i lang="pl">Wilno</i>), for 18 months the entity served as a <a href="/wiki/Buffer_state" title="Buffer state">buffer state</a> between <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, upon which it depended, and Lithuania, which claimed the area.<sup id="cite_ref-von_Rauch_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-von_Rauch-62">[60]</a></sup> After a variety of delays, <a href="/wiki/1922_Republic_of_Central_Lithuania_general_election" title="1922 Republic of Central Lithuania general election">a disputed election</a> took place on 8 January 1922, and the territory was annexed to Poland. Żeligowski later in his memoir which was published in London in 1943 condemned the annexation of the Republic by Poland, as well as the policy of closing Belarusian schools and general disregard of Marshal <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Józef Piłsudski">Józef Piłsudski</a>'s confederation plans by Polish ally.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> Years earlier interrogation report of 19-year-old revolutionary <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Józef Piłsudski">Pilsudski</a> of 10 March 1887 indicated that he called himself a "Belarusian nobleman".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1919 a part of Belarus under Russian rule emerged as the <a href="/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic</a> (Byelorussian SSR). Soon thereafter it merged to form the <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian%E2%80%93Belorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithuanian–Belorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">Lithuanian-Byelorussian SSR</a>. The contested lands were divided between Poland and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> after the war ended in 1921, and the Byelorussian SSR became a founding member of the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics in 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-birgerson_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birgerson-57">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> In the 1920s and 1930s, Soviet agricultural and economic policies, including <a href="/wiki/Collectivization" class="mw-redirect" title="Collectivization">collectivization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Five-Year_Plans_for_the_National_Economy_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Five-Year Plans for the National Economy of the Soviet Union">five-year plans for the national economy</a>, led to famine and political repression.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Western_Belorussia" title="Western Belorussia">western part of modern Belarus</a> remained part of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Second Polish Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ocu1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ocu1-67">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMinahan199837_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinahan199837-68">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> After an early period of liberalization, tensions between increasingly nationalistic Polish government and various increasingly separatist ethnic minorities started to grow, and the <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_minority_in_Poland" title="Belarusian minority in Poland">Belarusian minority</a> was no exception.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-70">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stosunki_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stosunki-71">[69]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Polonization" title="Polonization">polonization</a> drive was inspired and influenced by the Polish <a href="/wiki/National_Democracy" title="National Democracy">National Democracy</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Roman_Dmowski" title="Roman Dmowski">Roman Dmowski</a>, who advocated refusing Belarusians and Ukrainians the right for a free national development.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> A Belarusian organization, the <i><a href="/wiki/Belarusian_Peasants%27_and_Workers%27_Union" title="Belarusian Peasants' and Workers' Union">Belarusian Peasants' and Workers' Union</a></i>, was banned in 1927, and opposition to Polish government was met with state repressions.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-70">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stosunki_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stosunki-71">[69]</a></sup> Nonetheless compared to the (larger) <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_minority_in_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="Ukrainian minority in Poland">Ukrainian minority</a>, Belarusians were much less politically aware and active, and thus suffered fewer repressions than the Ukrainians.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-70">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stosunki_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stosunki-71">[69]</a></sup> In 1935, after the death of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Pi%C5%82sudski" title="Józef Piłsudski">Józef Piłsudski</a>, a new wave of repressions was released upon the minorities, with many <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox</a> churches and Belarusian schools being closed.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-70">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stosunki_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stosunki-71">[69]</a></sup> Use of the <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian language</a> was discouraged.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> Belarusian leadership was sent to <a href="/wiki/Bereza_Kartuska_prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Bereza Kartuska prison">Bereza Kartuska prison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War_and_later_Soviet_period">Second World War and later Soviet period</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Soviet_guerilla.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Soviet_guerilla.jpg/220px-Soviet_guerilla.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Soviet_guerilla.jpg/330px-Soviet_guerilla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Soviet_guerilla.jpg/440px-Soviet_guerilla.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1540" data-file-height="1016"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Soviet_guerilla.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Soviet_partisans#Belarus" title="Soviet partisans">Soviet partisan</a> fighters behind <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Front_line" title="Front line">front lines</a> in Belarus in 1943
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1939, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> and the Soviet Union invaded and occupied Poland, marking the beginning of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The Soviets invaded and annexed much of eastern Poland, which had been part of the country since the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Riga" title="Peace of Riga">Peace of Riga</a> two decades earlier. Much of the northern section of this area was added to the Byelorussian SSR, and now constitutes <a href="/wiki/West_Belarus" class="mw-redirect" title="West Belarus">West Belarus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-uni1_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni1-14">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uni2_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni2-15">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uni3_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni3-16">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uni4_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni4-75">[73]</a></sup> The Soviet-controlled Byelorussian People's Council officially took control of the territories, whose populations consisted of a mixture of Poles, Ukrainians, Belarusians and Jews, on 28 October 1939 in <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82ystok" title="Białystok">Białystok</a>. Nazi Germany <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">invaded the Soviet Union</a> in 1941. The <a href="/wiki/Defense_of_Brest_Fortress" title="Defense of Brest Fortress">defense</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brest_Fortress" title="Brest Fortress">Brest Fortress</a> was the first major battle of Operation Barbarossa. </p>
      <p>Statistically, the Byelorussian SSR was the hardest-hit Soviet republic in World War II; it <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Belarus_by_Nazi_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Occupation of Belarus by Nazi Germany">remained in Nazi hands</a> until 1944. During that time, Germany destroyed 209 out of 290 cities in the republic, 85% of the republic's industry, and more than one million buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-axell_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-axell-17">[15]</a></sup> The German <i lang="de" title="German-language text"><a href="/wiki/Generalplan_Ost" title="Generalplan Ost">Generalplan Ost</a></i> called for the extermination, expulsion, or enslavement of most or all Belarusians for the purpose of providing more <a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">living space</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Drang_nach_Osten" title="Drang nach Osten">East</a> for Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> Most of Western Belorussia became part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Reichskommissariat_Ostland" title="Reichskommissariat Ostland">Reichskommissariat Ostland</a></i> in 1941, but in 1943 the German authorities allowed local <a href="/wiki/Byelorussian_collaboration_with_Nazi_Germany" title="Byelorussian collaboration with Nazi Germany">collaborators</a> to set up a client state, the <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_Central_Rada" class="mw-redirect" title="Belarusian Central Rada">Belarusian Central Rada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The German occupation in 1941–1944 and war on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Eastern Front</a> devastated Belarus. More than 1.6 million civilians and 620,000 Belarusian <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a> soldiers died,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> and the country <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="World War II casualties of the Soviet Union">lost about 25% of its population</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Belarus" title="History of the Jews in Belarus">Jewish population of Belarus</a> was devastated during <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a> and never recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-axell_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-axell-17">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-warpop_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warpop-80">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> The population of Belarus did not regain its pre-war level until 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-warpop_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warpop-80">[78]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Khatyn_Memorial,_Belarus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Khatyn_Memorial%2C_Belarus.jpg/220px-Khatyn_Memorial%2C_Belarus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Khatyn_Memorial%2C_Belarus.jpg/330px-Khatyn_Memorial%2C_Belarus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Khatyn_Memorial%2C_Belarus.jpg/440px-Khatyn_Memorial%2C_Belarus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3750" data-file-height="2500"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Khatyn_Memorial,_Belarus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Khatyn" class="mw-redirect" title="Khatyn">Khatyn</a> Memorial; during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> the Germans murdered civilians in 5,295 different localities in <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Belarus_by_Nazi_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Occupation of Belarus by Nazi Germany">occupied Soviet Belarus</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the war, Belarus was among the 51 founding member states of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Charter">United Nations Charter</a> and as such it was allowed an additional vote at the UN, on top of the Soviet Union's vote. Vigorous postwar reconstruction promptly followed the end of the war and the Byelorussian SSR became a major center of manufacturing in the western USSR, creating jobs and attracting ethnic Russians.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The borders of the Byelorussian SSR and Poland were redrawn, in accord with the 1919-proposed <a href="/wiki/Curzon_Line" title="Curzon Line">Curzon Line</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-olson95_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-olson95-52">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> implemented a policy of <a href="/wiki/Sovietization" title="Sovietization">Sovietization</a> to isolate the Byelorussian SSR from <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western influences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-warpop_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warpop-80">[78]</a></sup> This policy involved sending Russians from various parts of the Soviet Union and placing them in key positions in the Byelorussian SSR government. After Stalin's death in 1953, <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> continued his predecessor's <a href="/wiki/Cultural_hegemony" title="Cultural hegemony">cultural hegemony</a> program, stating, "The sooner we all start speaking Russian, the faster we shall build communism."<sup id="cite_ref-warpop_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warpop-80">[78]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Soviet_people" title="Soviet people">Soviet</a> <a href="/wiki/Belarusians" title="Belarusians">Belarusian</a> communist politician <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Gromyko" title="Andrei Gromyko">Andrei Gromyko</a>, who served as Soviet foreign minister (1957–1985) and as <a href="/wiki/List_of_heads_of_state_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="List of heads of state of the Soviet Union">Chairman</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Presidium_of_the_Supreme_Soviet" title="Presidium of the Supreme Soviet">Presidium of the Supreme Soviet</a> (1985–1988), was responsible for many top decisions on Soviet foreign policy until he was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Shevardnadze" title="Eduard Shevardnadze">Eduard Shevardnadze</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> In 1986, the Byelorussian SSR was contaminated with most (70%) of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">nuclear fallout</a> from the explosion at the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl</a> power plant located 16&nbsp;km beyond the border in the neighboring <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Ukrainian Soviet Socialist Republic">Ukrainian SSR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gorby_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorby-84">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In June 1988, the archaeologist and leader of the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Christian_Party_%E2%80%93_BPF" title="Conservative Christian Party – BPF">Christian Conservative Party of the BPF</a> <a href="/wiki/Zyanon_Paznyak" class="mw-redirect" title="Zyanon Paznyak">Zyanon Paznyak</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Mass_grave" title="Mass grave">mass graves</a> of victims executed in 1937–41 at <a href="/wiki/Kurapaty" title="Kurapaty">Kurapaty</a>, near Minsk.<sup id="cite_ref-Gorby_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gorby-84">[82]</a></sup> Some nationalists contend that this discovery is proof that the Soviet government was trying to erase the Belarusian people, causing Belarusian nationalists to seek independence.<sup id="cite_ref-massgraves_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-massgraves-86">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_848095_Signing_the_Agreement_to_eliminate_the_USSR_and_establish_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/RIAN_archive_848095_Signing_the_Agreement_to_eliminate_the_USSR_and_establish_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_848095_Signing_the_Agreement_to_eliminate_the_USSR_and_establish_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/RIAN_archive_848095_Signing_the_Agreement_to_eliminate_the_USSR_and_establish_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_848095_Signing_the_Agreement_to_eliminate_the_USSR_and_establish_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/RIAN_archive_848095_Signing_the_Agreement_to_eliminate_the_USSR_and_establish_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_848095_Signing_the_Agreement_to_eliminate_the_USSR_and_establish_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1438" data-file-height="945"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_848095_Signing_the_Agreement_to_eliminate_the_USSR_and_establish_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Leaders of Russia, Ukraine and Belarus signed the <a href="/wiki/Belavezha_Accords" class="mw-redirect" title="Belavezha Accords">Belavezha Accords</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolving the Soviet Union</a>, 8 December 1991
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In March 1990, elections for seats in the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Soviet" title="Supreme Soviet">Supreme Soviet</a> of the Byelorussian SSR took place. Though the pro-independence <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_Popular_Front" title="Belarusian Popular Front">Belarusian Popular Front</a> took only 10% of the seats, the populace was content with the selection of the delegates.<sup id="cite_ref-byind_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byind-87">[85]</a></sup> Belarus declared itself sovereign on <span class="nowrap">27 July</span> 1990 by issuing the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_State_Sovereignty_of_the_Belarusian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Declaration of State Sovereignty of the Belarusian Soviet Socialist Republic">Declaration of State Sovereignty of the Belarusian Soviet Socialist Republic</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>With the support of the Communist Party, the country's name was changed to the Republic of Belarus on <span class="nowrap">25 August</span> 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-byind_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byind-87">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stanislau_Shushkevich" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanislau Shushkevich">Stanislav Shushkevich</a>, the chairman of the Supreme Soviet of Belarus, met with <a href="/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin" title="Boris Yeltsin">Boris Yeltsin</a> of Russia and <a href="/wiki/Leonid_Kravchuk" title="Leonid Kravchuk">Leonid Kravchuk</a> of Ukraine on <span class="nowrap">8 December</span> 1991 in <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82owie%C5%BCa_Forest" title="Białowieża Forest">Belavezhskaya Pushcha</a> to formally declare the dissolution of the Soviet Union and the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States" title="Commonwealth of Independent States">Commonwealth of Independent States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-byind_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byind-87">[85]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Belarus" title="Constitution of Belarus">national constitution</a> was adopted in March 1994 in which the functions of prime minister were given to the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Belarus" title="President of Belarus">President of Belarus</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Two-round elections for the presidency on (<span class="nowrap">24 June</span> 1994 and <span class="nowrap">10 July</span> 1994)<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> catapulted the formerly unknown <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko" title="Alexander Lukashenko">Alexander Lukashenko</a> into national prominence. He garnered 45% of the vote in the first round and 80%<sup id="cite_ref-byind_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byind-87">[85]</a></sup> in the second, defeating <a href="/wiki/Vyachaslau_Kebich" class="mw-redirect" title="Vyachaslau Kebich">Vyacheslav Kebich</a> who received 14% of the vote. Lukashenko was re-elected <a href="/wiki/2001_Belarusian_presidential_election" title="2001 Belarusian presidential election">in 2001</a>, <a href="/wiki/2006_Belarusian_presidential_election" title="2006 Belarusian presidential election">in 2006</a>, <a href="/wiki/2010_Belarusian_presidential_election" title="2010 Belarusian presidential election">in 2010</a> and again <a href="/wiki/2015_Belarusian_presidential_election" title="2015 Belarusian presidential election">in 2015</a>. Western governments,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-amnesty_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty-20">[18]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a><sup id="cite_ref-HRW_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HRW-90">[88]</a></sup> have criticized Lukashenko's <a href="/wiki/Authoritarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> style of government.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Lukashenko,_opening_of_Slavianski_Bazar_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alexander_Lukashenko%2C_opening_of_Slavianski_Bazar_2014.jpg/220px-Alexander_Lukashenko%2C_opening_of_Slavianski_Bazar_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alexander_Lukashenko%2C_opening_of_Slavianski_Bazar_2014.jpg/330px-Alexander_Lukashenko%2C_opening_of_Slavianski_Bazar_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alexander_Lukashenko%2C_opening_of_Slavianski_Bazar_2014.jpg/440px-Alexander_Lukashenko%2C_opening_of_Slavianski_Bazar_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3578" data-file-height="3000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Lukashenko,_opening_of_Slavianski_Bazar_2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko" title="Alexander Lukashenko">Alexander Lukashenko</a> has ruled Belarus since 1994.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Since 2014, following years of embrace of Russian influence in the country, Lukashenko has pressed a revival of Belarusian identity. For the first time, he delivered a speech in <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a> (rather than Russian, which most Belarusians speak as their primary language), in which he said, "We are not Russian—we are Belarusians", and later encouraged the use of Belarusian. Trade disputes, a border dispute, and a much relaxed official attitude to dissident voices are all part of a weakening of the longtime warm relationship with Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (August 2020)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In 2019, Lukashenko had bilateral talks in <a href="/wiki/Sochi" title="Sochi">Sochi</a> with Russian president <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> and declared that their two countries "could unite tomorrow, no problem."<sup id="cite_ref-moscow-times-bel_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moscow-times-bel-92">[90]</a></sup> An idea backed by Putin for years, observers have labeled the potential plan a scheme by Putin to remain in power beyond 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-newsweek-02-19_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newsweek-02-19-93">[91]</a></sup> However, political scientist Mikhail Vinogradov explained that "Lukashenko will play tough to the public while trying to look weak in front of Putin," and the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Moscow_Center" title="Carnegie Moscow Center">Carnegie Moscow Center</a>'s Artyom Shraibman suggested that "Moscow will most likely fail to find its base among Belarusians."<sup id="cite_ref-dw-02_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dw-02-94">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/2020%E2%80%9321_Belarusian_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2020–21 Belarusian protests">Mass protests</a> erupted across the country following the disputed <a href="/wiki/2020_Belarusian_presidential_election" title="2020 Belarusian presidential election">2020 Belarusian presidential election</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> in which Lukashenko sought a sixth term in office.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> Russian and EU leaders warned of any external interference in Belarus's internal affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> The Polish government has allotted a residence for main opposition candidate <a href="/wiki/Sviatlana_Tsikhanouskaya" title="Sviatlana Tsikhanouskaya">Sviatlana Tsikhanouskaya</a> and other members of the Belarusian opposition in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> Following the disputed election, Lukashenko is not recognized by the European Union and United States as the legitimate president of Belarus.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> The European Union and the United States recently imposed sanctions against Belarus because of the rigged election and political oppression during to the ongoing protests in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_climate">Geography and climate</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Belarus" title="Geography of Belarus">Geography of Belarus</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Belarus lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/51st_parallel_north" title="51st parallel north">51°</a> and <a href="/wiki/57th_parallel_north" title="57th parallel north">57° N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/23rd_meridian_east" title="23rd meridian east">23°</a> and <a href="/wiki/33rd_meridian_east" title="33rd meridian east">33° E</a>. Its extension from north to south is 560&nbsp;km (350&nbsp;mi), from west to east is 650&nbsp;km (400&nbsp;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-statistics_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statistics-105">[103]</a></sup> It is <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked</a>, relatively flat, and contains large tracts of <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshy</a> land.<sup id="cite_ref-ciageo_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ciageo-106">[104]</a></sup> About 40% of Belarus is covered by forests.<sup id="cite_ref-key_facts_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-key_facts-107">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> The country lies within two ecoregions: <a href="/wiki/Sarmatic_mixed_forests" title="Sarmatic mixed forests">Sarmatic mixed forests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_European_mixed_forests" title="Central European mixed forests">Central European mixed forests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DinersteinOlson2017_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DinersteinOlson2017-109">[107]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Many streams and 11,000 lakes are found in Belarus.<sup id="cite_ref-ciageo_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ciageo-106">[104]</a></sup> Three major rivers run through the country: the <a href="/wiki/Neman_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Neman River">Neman</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pripyat_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Pripyat River">Pripyat</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Dnieper_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Dnieper River">Dnieper</a>. The Neman flows westward towards the Baltic sea and the Pripyat flows eastward to the Dnieper; the Dnieper flows southward towards the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bell_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bell-110">[108]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Strusta_Lake_-_Panorama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Strusta_Lake_-_Panorama.jpg/220px-Strusta_Lake_-_Panorama.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="104" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Strusta_Lake_-_Panorama.jpg/330px-Strusta_Lake_-_Panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Strusta_Lake_-_Panorama.jpg/440px-Strusta_Lake_-_Panorama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4500" data-file-height="2136"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Strusta_Lake_-_Panorama.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Strusta_Lake" class="mw-redirect" title="Strusta Lake">Strusta Lake</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Vitebsk_Region" title="Vitebsk Region">Vitebsk Region</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The highest point is <a href="/wiki/Dzyarzhynskaya_Hara" title="Dzyarzhynskaya Hara">Dzyarzhynskaya Hara</a> (Dzyarzhynsk Hill) at 345 metres (1,132&nbsp;ft), and the lowest point is on the Neman River at 90&nbsp;m (295&nbsp;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-ciageo_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ciageo-106">[104]</a></sup> The average elevation of Belarus is 160&nbsp;m (525&nbsp;ft) <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> The climate features mild to cold winters, with January minimum temperatures ranging from −4&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a> (24.8&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">°F</a>) in southwest (<a href="/wiki/Brest,_Belarus" title="Brest, Belarus">Brest</a>) to −8&nbsp;°C (17.6&nbsp;°F) in northeast (<a href="/wiki/Vitebsk" title="Vitebsk">Vitebsk</a>), and cool and moist summers with an average temperature of 18&nbsp;°C (64.4&nbsp;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-locclimate_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locclimate-112">[110]</a></sup> Belarus has an average annual rainfall of 550 to 700&nbsp;mm (21.7 to 27.6&nbsp;in).<sup id="cite_ref-locclimate_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-locclimate-112">[110]</a></sup> The country is in the transitional zone between <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">continental climates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">maritime climates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ciageo_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ciageo-106">[104]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Natural resources include <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> deposits, small quantities of oil and natural gas, <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a>, dolomite (<a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>), <a href="/wiki/Marl" title="Marl">marl</a>, chalk, sand, gravel, and clay.<sup id="cite_ref-ciageo_106-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ciageo-106">[104]</a></sup> About 70% of the radiation from neighboring Ukraine's 1986 <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl nuclear disaster</a> entered Belarusian territory, and about a fifth of Belarusian land (principally farmland and forests in the southeastern regions) was affected by radiation fallout.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> The United Nations and other agencies have aimed to reduce the level of radiation in affected areas, especially through the use of <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> binders and <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">rapeseed</a> cultivation, which are meant to decrease soil levels of <a href="/wiki/Caesium-137" title="Caesium-137">caesium-137</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-uncher_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uncher-114">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iaea_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaea-115">[113]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Belarus borders five countries: <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> to the north and the east, and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> to the south. Treaties in 1995 and 1996 demarcated Belarus's borders with Latvia and Lithuania, and Belarus ratified a 1997 treaty establishing the Belarus-Ukraine border in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> Belarus and Lithuania ratified final border demarcation documents in February 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup> </p>
